Prompts

AI dictionary

[View demo](https://x.com/zarazhangrui/status/1991047223715770803?s=20)

Build an AI dictionary with these features.

User first select their native language and target language (for each, options include the 10 most popular languages in the world).

User can input with text (word, phrase, sentence).

The result displays a natural language definition in the user's native language, as well as an AI-generated image visualizing the concept. It also includes 2 example sentences, each followed by a translation in the user's native language. It also includes an usage note, which explains the cultural nuance, how it's used, tone/voice, or related words (synonyms/words that look similar and are often confused) and how they differ in usage. The explanation should be fun, lively, and in casual language, like you're talking to a friend. Don't sound like a textbook. Be concise. No greetings or fillers; get to the point immediately.

For each word or phrase defined, provide a button that plays a clear audio recording of its pronunciation in the target language. Make sure it sounds natural, not robotic. All example sentences should also have a pronunciation feature. Make sure the audio latency is low.

The user can choose to save any result page to their "Notebook" for review later.

In the Notebook, there's a feature where the AI can "make up a story" using the words/phrases contained in the notebook, in order to help the user memorize them.

There's a "Study" mode, where the AI generates flashcards for words in the notebook, where the front is the word in the target language against the image that represents the word, and the back is the word and its definition in native language (along with example sentence). When they click on the card, there should be a flipping animation.

Make it mobile-optimized. Overall vibe is bright and fun. Make it pop.

Video recorder with live prompting

[View demo](https://x.com/zarazhangrui/status/1991009823614513479?s=20)

A web app that hooks up to the user's camera that lets users record videos of themselves talking to the camera. 
  
  
The AI should display good prompts (questions) to get the user's creative juices flowing so they know what to talk about.
  
  
After recording, the user should be able to download the video as a mp4 file.
  
  
Also allow the user to choose dimension of video (9:16, 16:9, 3:4, 1:1).
  
  
Have Gemini actively listen in on what the user is saying and jump in with live questions at various points (as overlay text only, no audio) so the user is never stuck, so it's like the AI is a video podcast host interviewing the user.
  
  
Use pastel colors and make the design classy. Make sure the UI auto-adjusts for all video layouts.

Acquired Podcast to book

[View demo](https://x.com/zarazhangrui/status/2009080125292511655?s=20)

**Your Role & Mission**

You are my executive assistant helping me transform a long Acquired podcast episode into a compelling written chapter for a physical book I'm creating. Think of yourself as a skilled ghostwriter who listens to the entire episode and crafts it into something that reads like a chapter from a classic business biography.

**Style & Voice**

Write like a chapter from a great business biography--think *Shoe Dog*, *The Everything Store*, or *Hatching Twitter*. This means:

- Rich narrative with dramatic tension

- Key turning points treated as pivotal scenes

- Quotes woven in to let the protagonists speak for themselves

- The reader should feel like they're watching history unfold, not reading a summary

- Analytical insight layered into the storytelling, not separated from it

**Length**

As long as the story warrants. Use your judgment based on the episode's length and insight density. Quality and completeness over brevity. This is meant to be a satisfying read, not a skim.

**Step 1: Understand the Arc**

Listen to/read the full episode and identify:

- The central narrative: What is the story being told? What's the dramatic question?

- The key characters: Who are the protagonists, antagonists, and supporting players?

- The turning points: What are the 3-5 moments where everything changed?

- The stakes: What was at risk? What could have gone wrong?

Great business stories have narrative shape--a beginning that sets the stage, rising tension, pivotal decisions, and resolution (or ongoing cliffhanger). Find that shape.

**Step 2: Map the Characters**

Acquired episodes often feature many players, which can be disorienting. Solve this for the reader by:

- Introducing each character clearly on first appearance with a brief identifying detail (role, relationship to the central figure, why they matter)

- Re-anchoring the reader when a character reappears after a gap (e.g., "Sculley--the Pepsi executive Jobs had personally recruited--now faced an impossible choice")

- Keeping the focus on the 3-5 most important figures; mention minor characters only when necessary and don't let them clutter the narrative

- Using consistent identifiers (if you call someone "the young engineer" once, don't switch to "the Stanford grad" later without reason)

The reader should never have to stop and ask "Wait, who is this again?"

**Step 3: Identify and Explain "Blocker" Concepts**

Scan for business, technical, or industry-specific concepts that are essential to understanding the story. These are "blocker" concepts--if the reader doesn't understand them, they'll be lost.

For each blocker concept:

- Explain it in plain language using an analogy or real-world example

- Keep explanations to 1-2 sentences maximum

- Weave these explanations naturally into the narrative the first time the concept appears

- Use web search where necessary to ensure accuracy when explaining technical concepts

The target reader is someone who is generally intelligent and curious--they read business books and follow tech news, but they may not know the specifics of every industry. Think of someone with a liberal arts degree who's interested in how great companies are built.

**Step 4: Harvest the Best Quotes**

Acquired episodes are rich with two types of quotes--preserve both:

**From the hosts (Ben Gilbert and David Rosenthal):**

- Their sharpest analytical insights and observations

- Memorable one-liners or turns of phrase

- Moments where they reveal something surprising or counterintuitive

- Attribute clearly (e.g., "As David Rosenthal puts it..." or "Ben Gilbert observes that...")

**From primary sources the hosts cite:**

- Quotes from founders, executives, journalists, biographers

- Historical documents, memos, interviews they reference

- These are gold--they let the protagonists speak for themselves

- Attribute clearly (e.g., "As Jobs later recalled..." or "In a memo to the board, Hastings wrote...")

When cleaning up quotes:

- Remove filler words (um, uh, like, you know)

- Fix grammatical mistakes from natural speech

- Keep the speaker's authentic voice and meaning intact

- Longer quotes are fine if they're powerful--this isn't a tight summary

**Step 5: Build the Narrative**

Structure the piece like a chapter from a biography:

**Opening:** Start with a scene, a tension, or a question that pulls the reader in. Drop them into a pivotal moment, then zoom out to set the stage. Avoid "This episode covers..." framing--just begin the story.

**Middle:** Move through the narrative chronologically or thematically, depending on what serves the story best. Treat major turning points as scenes--slow down, add detail, let the reader feel the weight of the moment. Use quotes to let key players speak at crucial junctures.

**Closing:** End with resonance--what happened next, what it meant, what lesson or question lingers. The reader should close the chapter feeling like they understand something important about business, strategy, or human nature.

**Step 6: Weave It Together**

Combine narrative, analysis, and quotes into one flowing piece that:

- Reads like a chapter from a great business book, not a podcast summary

- Has no section headers, bullet points, or artificial breaks (a line break between major sections is fine)

- Includes a compelling title in the style of a book chapter

- At the beginning, includes a short paragraph capturing the essence of the story and why it matters

- Makes complete sense to someone who has never heard the podcast

- Is designed to be printed--no links or screen-dependent elements

- Balances storytelling with insight: the reader should be both entertained and educated

**Quality Check:**

Before you finish, ask yourself:

1. "Does this read like a chapter from a business book I'd actually want to read?"

2. "Does the opening pull me in immediately, like a great first page?"

3. "Have I preserved the best quotes from both the hosts and the primary sources they cite?"

4. "Do the turning points land with dramatic weight, or did I rush past them?"

5. "Would someone who knows nothing about this company walk away understanding the story and why it matters?"

6. "Can the reader keep track of who's who throughout the piece?"

7. "Would this print beautifully in a physical book?"

If yes to all, you've succeeded.

X analytics dashboard in HTML

[View demo](https://x.com/zarazhangrui/status/2018103976668143878?s=20)

I want to visualize my X/Twitter growth and get content analysis. I have two CSV files from X Analytics:

1. **Account Overview CSV** (contains daily follower data: Date, New follows, Unfollows)

2. **Content Analytics CSV** (contains post performance: Impressions, Likes, Engagements, Post text, URLs)

Please create an interactive HTML dashboard that includes:

## 1. FOLLOWER GROWTH CHART

- Interactive line chart showing follower count over time

- Filter to start from when I began posting regularly (I'll tell you the date)

- Mark milestone achievements (10K, 20K, 30K, 40K etc.) with vertical lines and labels at the top

- Mark my biggest viral days with annotations showing the date and follower gain

- Hover tooltips showing exact follower count and daily net change

- Stats cards showing: Total Growth, Average Daily Net, Best Single Day, Days Tracked

## 2. TOP 10 POSTS OF ALL TIME

- Ranked by impressions

- Show: rank, impressions, likes, new followers gained

- Include the post text (or summary if too long)

- Link to each post

## 3. CONTENT ANALYSIS

Analyze my top posts and provide:

**Why They Went Viral**: Identify 3-4 patterns in what made my best posts succeed

**Content-Market Fit**: A one-sentence positioning statement for my content + who I serve + what gap I fill

**My Unique Thought Patterns**: 3-4 recurring themes or approaches in how I think/write

**Future Topic Suggestions**:

- "Double Down" topics (proven winners to do more of)

- "Expand Into" topics (adjacent territories)

- "Experiment With" topics (higher risk ideas)

**Ready-to-Tweet Ideas**: 12 concrete tweet drafts organized by category, based on what works for me

## DESIGN REQUIREMENTS

- Clean, modern design (not generic AI aesthetic)

- Use a cohesive color palette

- Mobile-responsive

- No external dependencies except Chart.js (load from CDN)

- Single HTML file I can open locally

## MY DATA

[Paste your Account Overview CSV here]

[Paste your Content Analytics CSV here]

My current follower count: [YOUR NUMBER]

Date I want to start tracking from: [YOUR DATE]

If you’ve ever said ‘I want to learn AI,’ this is your go-to list. My friend Zara Zhang created a curated AI learning list she’s personally used and tested, She’s not from a technical background,  which makes this guide perfect for anyone getting started with AI.   For years, we’ve been told to “learn new skills” to stay relevant. But with AI, it’s not just what you learn — it’s how you learn that’s changing. Today, the best skill you can build isn’t coding, prompt writing, or mastering every new tool, it’s adaptability. Because the people who can connect ideas, ask smart questions, and think creatively with AI will always stay ahead. If you’re starting out, get curious and experiment and if you’re mid-career, unlearn the idea that expertise means knowing everything. In this new era, your ability to evolve is your real competitive edge.   Everyone’s chasing success... but real growth? It’s all about continuous learning. Here’s what I’ve learned from spending few years building AI systems and experimenting with what actually works in AI practice: 1. Small steps each day beat “overnight” efforts. 2. Real understanding comes from hands-on experiments (and tech failures!). 3. Sharing your journey inspires others and deepens your own expertise. Remember, folks, it’s learning, sharing, and iterating that grow both careers and communities. * [Nitesh Gupta](https://jp.linkedin.com/in/niteshgupta2?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  Senior DevOps/Product Security Engineer @ PayPay Corporation | Ex Philips | Ex NTT |AWS, GCP, CICD,K8S,Security,Crowdstrike,Snyk, AI Agent building, cursor, Claude etc.

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fniteshgupta2_ai-artificialintelligence-lifelonglearning-activity-7393595599587217408-jSJY&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Unlock Your Brain's Potential with AI Anyone else feel like they're constantly playing catch-up with the rate of change these days? It's wild how quickly new technologies and information are flooding in. I used to feel overwhelmed, like I was always behind. It's not a good feeling, especially in a field that demands you constantly acquire new skills to stay relevant. Then I started experimenting with AI tools to help me learn \*smarter\*, not just harder. And the results have been pretty impressive. It's not about replacing traditional learning methods, not at all. For me, it's about augmenting them, making them more efficient and personalized. For example, I've been using AI to summarize dense research papers and articles. Instead of slogging through pages of jargon, I get a concise overview of the key findings. This lets me quickly decide if something is truly relevant to my work or not, saving me a ton of time. ⏱️ Another thing I've found super helpful is using AI to generate practice questions on topics I'm studying. It's like having a personalized quiz master at my disposal. This helps me identify gaps in my understanding and focus my efforts where they're needed most. I'm a big believer in learning by doing, and this has been a game-changer. I also love how AI can adapt to my learning style. Some tools can identify my strengths and weaknesses and tailor the learning content accordingly. It’s like having a personal tutor who understands how my brain works. 🧠 Of course, it's important to be critical and not blindly trust everything AI spits out. I always double-check the information and use my own judgment. Think of it as a powerful assistant, not a replacement for your own critical thinking. I'm still early on this journey, but I'm excited about the potential of AI to revolutionize the way we learn. It's about empowering ourselves to keep up with the ever-changing world around us. What are your thoughts? Have you been using AI to boost your learning? I'd love to hear about your experiences! 👇 [#AI](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Fai&trk=public_post-text) [#ArtificialIntelligence](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Fartificialintelligence&trk=public_post-text) [#LifelongLearning](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Flifelonglearning&trk=public_post-text) [#SkillsDevelopment](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Fskillsdevelopment&trk=public_post-text) [#FutureofWork](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Ffutureofwork&trk=public_post-text)

* [Vidhi Jain](https://in.linkedin.com/in/vidhijain-26vj?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fvidhijain-26vj_artificialintelligence-learningwithai-activity-7388220392173191168-Zkwg&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  💡 𝗧𝗵𝗲 𝘀𝗺𝗮𝗿𝘁𝗲𝘀𝘁 𝗱𝗲𝗰𝗶𝘀𝗶𝗼𝗻 𝗜 𝗺𝗮𝗱𝗲 𝘁𝗵𝗶𝘀 𝘆𝗲𝗮𝗿 – 𝗹𝗲𝗮𝗿𝗻𝗶𝗻𝗴 𝗵𝗼𝘄 𝘁𝗼 𝘄𝗼𝗿𝗸 𝘄𝗶𝘁𝗵 𝗔𝗜, 𝗻𝗼𝘁 𝗮𝗴𝗮𝗶𝗻𝘀𝘁 𝗶𝘁. A few months ago, I used AI just like everyone else for small, random tasks. But this year, something changed. I started really exploring it. I’ve always been curious about technology, and seeing how AI is evolving made me want to experiment with it myself. What I realized is simple but powerful: 𝘼𝙄 𝙙𝙤𝙚𝙨𝙣’𝙩 𝙧𝙚𝙥𝙡𝙖𝙘𝙚 𝙮𝙤𝙪𝙧 𝙘𝙧𝙚𝙖𝙩𝙞𝙫𝙞𝙩𝙮, 𝙞𝙩 𝙝𝙚𝙡𝙥𝙨 𝙮𝙤𝙪 𝙚𝙭𝙥𝙧𝙚𝙨𝙨 𝙞𝙩 𝙗𝙚𝙩𝙩𝙚𝙧. It’s not just a tool that gives you answers — it’s something that helps you 𝙩𝙝𝙞𝙣𝙠 𝙗𝙚𝙩𝙩𝙚𝙧, 𝙘𝙧𝙚𝙖𝙩𝙚 𝙗𝙚𝙩𝙩𝙚𝙧, 𝙖𝙣𝙙 𝙚𝙭𝙥𝙧𝙚𝙨𝙨 𝙗𝙚𝙩𝙩𝙚𝙧. 🤖 Here’s how I’ve been using AI in my daily life: 1️⃣ Content creation: It helps me organize my ideas and present them clearly. 2️⃣ Brainstorming: When I’m stuck, it gives me a spark — not a shortcut. 3️⃣ Studies: It summarizes and simplifies my notes so I can focus on learning. 4️⃣ Idea validation: Helps me test and refine my thoughts before sharing. 5️⃣ Curiosity & learning: The more I explore, the more I grow — not just in skills, but in mindset. 💭 The biggest thing I’ve learned:- AI doesn’t steal creativity it amplifies it. Ideas still come from us our emotions, our experiences, our understanding. AI just helps us express them more effectively. This curiosity to explore, learn, and experiment with AI has been one of the most fulfilling parts of my year. Because honestly, working with AI has taught me as much about technology as it has about myself. 🌱 What about you has AI changed the way you think or create? * [RICHMAN O.](https://ng.linkedin.com/in/richman-o-145424122?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Frichman-o-145424122_this-will-run-into-issues-mid-course-even-activity-7389269412689731584-8cus&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  This will run into issues mid-course, even with the best models. A few tricks to make it seamless: 1️⃣ Save the main prompt as [instructions.md](https://www.linkedin.com/redir/redirect?url=http%3A%2F%2Finstructions%2Emd&urlhash=4Gqu&trk=public_post-text). 2️⃣ Open a new chat and use that prompt to generate a detailed course outline — focus on the outcome you want at the end. Paint the picture clearly. 3️⃣ Save that outline as [course-outline.md](https://www.linkedin.com/redir/redirect?url=http%3A%2F%2Fcourse-outline%2Emd&urlhash=agq1&trk=public_post-text) (you can merge both into one file, but make sure to use clear headers). Then, load the file into a project and start a new chat for the course itself. This way, the model maintains shared context while staying anchored to the original course goal you defined at the beginning. Now you can ask questions freely without losing context.

  [Lucy Njuguna](https://ke.linkedin.com/in/lucynjuguna?trk=public_post_reshare_feed-actor-name)

  AI Automation Specialist | E-commerce Operations Automation | Scaling Online Stores Without Adding Staff | AI & No-Code Tools

  The truth about AI and learning that nobody’s really talking about. We’re all doing the same thing, Reading books. Watching videos. Saving quotes. But for real… most of it never sticks. We’re still learning like it’s 2010. Taking notes, highlighting, nodding along... then forgetting it all a week later. Meanwhile, AI is reshaping everything; How we work, think, create, even how we learn. But most people are still pretending they don’t need it. Here’s the uncomfortable truth: Using AI to learn isn’t cheating. It’s adapting. Think about it. You read something deep. You highlight it. You move on. Then it fades into the pile of “things I once found inspiring.” Now imagine this instead: You drop that same quote into an AI chat. You ask follow-up questions. You break it down until it makes sense to you. You get examples that actually fit your world. That’s not laziness. That’s smart learning. Old way: Read → Forget → Repeat New way: Read → Question → Apply → Remember AI doesn’t think for you. It helps you think better. I’ve been experimenting with this, and it’s changed how I learn. I don’t just consume anymore. I interact with ideas. I even built a simple prompt that turns AI into a personal tutor. Five steps. Five minutes. Five times deeper than just reading. Because the truth is, AI already shapes how we think; through feeds, algorithms, and even recommendations. So the question isn’t if it’s influencing you. It’s how intentionally you’re using it. Next time you find something that moves you, don’t just highlight it. Drop it into AI. Ask, “What does this actually mean for me?” Because the future of personal growth isn’t about what you learn.... It’s about how fast you can turn learning into action. Start learning like it’s 2025, not 2010. Try the prompt, talk to your ideas, and see how fast your understanding compounds. * [Maddie Robinson](https://www.linkedin.com/in/robinsonmaddie?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Frobinsonmaddie_ai-technology-innovation-activity-7390026510050451456-xlVk&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Technology won’t replace you. Failing to adapt will. I hear it all the time these days: “AI is going to replace me.” The truth is, it’s not. All the technology evolving around us still depends on humans to operate it. It’s only as powerful as the creative, free-thinking mind guiding it. The irony is that AI itself is a monument to human innovation. Every algorithm and data model is built from ideas, insights, and discoveries that came from people. If anything, technology doesn’t replace us: it reflects us. It enhances us. It amplifies our curiosity, creativity, and capacity to learn. ...But only if we let it. That’s why I encourage you to challenge yourself to learn how to use these tools. Explore them. Experiment with them. Let them teach you. You can even use AI to teach yourself how to use AI! You can make it your own personal assistant: one that helps you think, plan, write, learn, and create faster than ever before. You can use it to brainstorm ideas, automate repetitive tasks, analyze data, or simplify complex topics in seconds. We now live in an age where you can create a 24/7 tutor, build custom tools that fit your workflow, and learn about any subject that sparks your curiosity. The only limit is how willing you are to explore it. ...So maybe the real question isn’t whether AI will replace us, but whether we’ll choose to evolve alongside it. If you’re looking for good places to start, there are incredible creators out there breaking down how to actually use these tools. Many share tutorials, prompts, and workflows that make learning accessible to anyone curious enough to try. [Ruben Hassid](https://il.linkedin.com/in/ruben-hassid?trk=public_post-text), [Anisha Jain](https://uk.linkedin.com/in/theanishajain?trk=public_post-text) are two of my favorites right now. * [Aadhar Chaturvedi](https://in.linkedin.com/in/aadhar-chaturvedi?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Faadhar-chaturvedi_artificialintelligence-futureofwork-digitalindia-activity-7395673835145986048-Q4FP&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Yesterday, something small happened that changed how I look at AI forever. My house help’s son, a Class 8 kid, came to me with a school project on climate change. He looked stressed. English was not his strong suit, and he had no idea how to present his research. Instead of writing it for him, I opened my laptop and showed him how to ask AI the right question. He didn’t blink. His eyes softened. And then he said something I didn’t expect, “𝘐𝘴 𝘵𝘩𝘪𝘴, 𝘢𝘭𝘭𝘰𝘸𝘦𝘥 𝘧𝘰𝘳 𝘴𝘰𝘮𝘦𝘰𝘯𝘦 𝘭𝘪𝘬𝘦 𝘮𝘦?” That line hit harder than anything an algorithm could generate. Someone like me. A kid who’s grown up believing that knowledge belongs to a different world. ▪️ That English is a barrier. ▪️ That learning comes with conditions. ▪️ That opportunity has a price tag. In that moment, AI was not a “tool.” It was a door. We refined it together, he adjusted the summary, added examples, and even asked the AI to explain the science “like I am 12.” Not only he walked out with a solid presentation, but he walked out with something far bigger. A tool he can use for the rest of his life. And here is what hit me. AI is not replacing adults in offices. It is reshaping opportunity for kids in small homes. It is reducing the gap between those who “know” and those who “can learn.” It is not about being smarter, it is about knowing how to ask better. One moment of access. One tiny spark. One new direction for someone who never imagined he could compete. That is the real impact of AI not in boardrooms, but in everyday lives we rarely notice. But the real question is, 👉 Are you using AI just to finish tasks? Or to expand what you believe is possible?   🚀 Meet Zyvora — My AI that loves to learn how you learn! 🤖💡 Ever wished your study app actually understood you before throwing a bunch of formulas at your face? Well, that’s exactly what Zyvora does. 🧠 The name comes from: “Zy” – futuristic and adaptive, “vora” – inspired by “to devour” (because Zyvora literally devours knowledge). 🎯 What does it do? Zyvora analyses how students understand different concepts through interactive surveys. Based on your responses, it adapts its approach — helping you learn smarter, not harder. Think of it as your personal AI tutor that says, “Don’t worry, I got you!” instead of “You should’ve revised that last night.” 😅 👨💻 Built with passion, curiosity, and a pinch of caffeine, Zyvora aims to make learning more personal, engaging, and (dare I say it?) fun! * [Jason Liu](https://ca.linkedin.com/in/jason-liu-45b73220?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fjason-liu-45b73220_ai-artificialintelligence-futureofwork-activity-7394089694919102464-M9Fp&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  What I’ve Learned About Working With AI Lately, I’ve realized that the way we work with AI should be very different from how we used to work before. At first, I treated AI like a better version of Google — something that could refine my questions and give me a direct answer, so I didn’t have to go through pages of search results. That alone was already great. And when it started coding for me in seconds, it felt even better. But I soon noticed a drawback: while AI could produce technically correct results, it often missed the big picture of what was in my mind. The code worked, but sometimes the logic or idea didn’t fit. You might say, “the prompt is the key.” Sure — but honestly, I don’t want to learn some special “prompt engineering” skill just to talk to a tool. The whole point of AI should be that it’s simple and accessible for everyone. What I’ve learned, though, is this: 👉 Don’t treat AI like Google or just a coding assistant. Treat it like a partner — a helper, a consultant, a creative companion. When I started working with AI on my real-time assistant project, I wasn’t an expert in audio or video processing — just had some basic knowledge. Normally, that would mean spending weeks or months learning, reading books, or hiring specialists. But working alongside AI, I was able to: Start the project immediately. Learn only what I needed to know, exactly when I needed it. Build something functional while gaining new skills on the go. This changed the way I see the future of work and education. We might not need to spend years mastering one narrow field before we can create something valuable. Instead, the future belongs to those who can think, design, and create — using AI as an extension of their knowledge. AI isn’t just a tool. It’s a bridge between imagination and reality. Title: AI Learning Resource for Non-Technical Professionals by Zara Zhang
URL: https://www.linkedin.com/posts/yi-elva-l-22524a64_sharing-a-well-curated-ai-learning-resource-activity-7408608204005433344-XYVM
Content: Sharing a well-curated AI learning resource for non-technical people.  These resources are put together by Zara Zhang . She’s not a software engineer, but a strong learn-in-public practitioner around AI products. This makes her content much more accessible to people like me. As an absolute beginner, I’ve really enjoyed (and would highly recommend) starting with the videos. In simple terms, industry professionals explain the basics of AI models, how to write good prompts, and the design philosophy behind early products. It’s true that the most experienced people often explain complex ideas the most clearly. From the tools recommends, I’m currently exploring NotebookLM, which is great for: 1. rough learning on difficult or “dry” subjects (for example, turning 10 years of public annual [...] needs a solution a video idea forces me to understand something deeply a project breaks and I need answers That’s when learning sticks. 4️⃣ Follow people, not noise A few trusted voices > dozens of trend accounts. That’s why I created a newsletter - to share only the most important updates, without the noise. AI moves fast, but not everything is urgent. This is my way of learning - what’s yours? Let me know in the comments! [...] apply it faster, and see real value faster. The internet may offer a million directions for AI. But make it practical, make it human, make it theirs— And people are all in.
Raw Content: [Yi (Elva) L.](https://uk.linkedin.com/in/yi-elva-l-22524a64?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

* [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fyi-elva-l-22524a64_sharing-a-well-curated-ai-learning-resource-activity-7408608204005433344-XYVM&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

Sharing a well-curated AI learning resource for non-technical people. [https://zara.faces.site/ai](https://www.linkedin.com/redir/redirect?url=https%3A%2F%2Fzara%2Efaces%2Esite%2Fai&urlhash=T5D-&trk=public_post-text) These resources are put together by Zara Zhang . She’s not a software engineer, but a strong learn-in-public practitioner around AI products. This makes her content much more accessible to people like me. As an absolute beginner, I’ve really enjoyed (and would highly recommend) starting with the videos. In simple terms, industry professionals explain the basics of AI models, how to write good prompts, and the design philosophy behind early products. It’s true that the most experienced people often explain complex ideas the most clearly. From the tools recommends, I’m currently exploring NotebookLM, which is great for: 1. rough learning on difficult or “dry” subjects (for example, turning 10 years of public annual reports into a personal podcast) 2. pre-curating learnings on one specific topic from different media formats. Learning AI this way reminds me a lot of how I approach commodities: start from practitioners, build intuition, and then go deeper over time. If you end up exploring it, let me know what you think.

  Here’s a fun plot twist. In the world of AI, there are no experts, only learners. Tools change too fast. Best practices evolve too quickly. What’s “cutting-edge” today feels outdated three releases later. So instead of chasing full proficiency, let's chase curiosity. The competitive advantage isn’t knowing everything—it’s being willing to keep learning. Because in the age of AI, expertise isn’t a finish line. It’s a habit.

* [Bushra Akram](https://pk.linkedin.com/in/bushra-akram-3ba49a279?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fbushra-akram-3ba49a279_comparison-almost-made-me-quit-learning-ai-activity-7407286485894225920-jY2P&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Comparison almost made me quit learning AI. While learning AI, I constantly compared: • my progress with others • my understanding with experts • my pace with social media timelines It silently killed my confidence. What I learned the hard way: AI is not a race. It’s a long-term skill-building process. The moment I stopped comparing and started focusing on: → my own learning pace → small implementations → documenting what I learn Everything changed. I started understanding concepts better. I gained confidence. And people began resonating with my journey. 📌 Important lesson for anyone learning AI: You don’t need to be the fastest. You need to be consistent. Personal branding doesn’t come from pretending to be perfect. It comes from showing up honestly. If you’re building your AI career and learning in public, let’s connect.

* [Koshik Mehta](https://in.linkedin.com/in/koshik-mehta-9271b0258?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fkoshik-mehta-9271b0258_i-spent-50k-on-ai-courses-and-learned-activity-7411250881981116416-sruo&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  I spent ₹50k on AI courses and learned… almost nothing. Not because the content was bad. But because I was consuming, not building. Course after course. Notes after notes. Buzzwords in my head. Zero confidence in my hands. I knew the terms. LLMs. RAG. Agents. But ask me to ship something? Blank. That’s when it clicked. Learning AI is not about information. It’s about friction. What I was doing wrong back then: I was waiting to “feel ready”. Watching tutorials end to end like Netflix. Collecting certificates like achievements. Avoiding messy projects because they scared me. What actually changed everything: Building small, broken things. Letting AI debug my mistakes, not copy answers. Shipping ugly versions without overthinking. Learning only when I was stuck. The learning curve isn’t steep. It’s misleading. AI makes everything look easy… until you try to apply it to a real problem. And that struggle? It’s not failure. It’s proof you’re finally doing real work. So if you’re confused right now good. That’s exactly where progress starts.

* [Richard SA Hurtley](https://uk.linkedin.com/in/richardhurtley?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Frichardhurtley_someone-recommended-me-a-book-on-ai-i-built-activity-7404788310142824448-HTFE&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Someone recommended me a book on AI. I built a learning engine instead. Here’s what happened: Colleague: “You should read this book on AI’s origins.” Me: \*Opens Claude instead\* Me (typing): “I want to understand the AI origins topic but don’t have time to read multiple books right now. Can you help me create something I can listen to to get some topic context?” (Claude didn’t just answer. It optimised my prompt and built me a system.) 30 seconds later → Comprehensive synthesis from multiple expert perspectives to offer topic overview 5 minutes later → NotebookLM converts it into a conversational podcast 30 minute commute → Podcast topic overview listened to Time invested: 35 minutes total. This isn’t about replacing books as I will likely read 1 or 2 anyway when I have time. It’s about stacking tools to match how you actually learn. Claude gives you the synthesis. NotebookLM gives you the format. You get the contextual understanding. The real skill isn’t using AI. It’s knowing which AI to use when, and ultimately how to make them work together. Most people are still using these tools in isolation. You could be building a system instead. Comment “PODCAST” for a summary 15-min hosted MP4 audio.

* [Louis-François Bouchard](https://ca.linkedin.com/in/whats-ai?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fwhats-ai_here-are-my-4-ways-to-keep-learning-ai-while-activity-7414002972063600640-v_JB&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  Here are my 4 ways to keep learning AI while running a company (without burning out) 👇 Staying “up to date” in AI does not mean reading everything, testing every tool, or chasing every new model. That’s the fastest way to get overwhelmed. Here’s what actually works for me: 1️⃣ Ignore most things You don’t need to be first. If something really important, it will come back again and again through people you trust. 2️⃣ Let relevance filter itself I pay attention to: what my team is using what other practitioners keep mentioning what actually works in real projects If it doesn’t show up there, I don’t touch it. 3️⃣ Learn only when there’s a reason I don’t “schedule learning time.” Learning happens naturally when: a client problem needs a solution a video idea forces me to understand something deeply a project breaks and I need answers That’s when learning sticks. 4️⃣ Follow people, not noise A few trusted voices > dozens of trend accounts. That’s why I created a newsletter - to share only the most important updates, without the noise. AI moves fast, but not everything is urgent. This is my way of learning - what’s yours? Let me know in the comments!

* [Shafali R. Anand](https://in.linkedin.com/in/shafali-r-anand?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fshafali-r-anand_responsible-use-of-ai-what-it-means-for-activity-7407272401039912960-HtOP&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  I am seeing a heartening surge in posts about writers attempting to wrest the control back from AI, and this brings me the responsible use of AI. What exactly is responsible use? I think it is, "using AI to do the right task, at the right time, in the right measure, without letting it erode our own ability to think and create." Can we stop using AI altogether? Honest answer: No. We must use AI for certain tasks, or risk becoming irrelevant, even unemployed. So, what can we do? Don't use AI for everything - only for things that you can't avoid using it for. Use it only for the tasks for which AI use is "Required" and "Acceptable." Using it for all the tasks, including the ones that you are passionate about, could be "Dangerous." More info on "RAD - The 3 Baskets of AI": [https://lnkd.in/gZzigDMA](https://www.linkedin.com/redir/redirect?url=https%3A%2F%2Flnkd%2Ein%2FgZzigDMA&urlhash=YJEB&trk=public_post-text) [#instructionaldesigners](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Finstructionaldesigners&trk=public_post-text) [#teachers](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Fteachers&trk=public_post-text) [#trainers](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Ftrainers&trk=public_post-text) [#responsibleAI](https://www.linkedin.com/signup/cold-join?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Ffeed%2Fhashtag%2Fresponsibleai&trk=public_post-text)

* [Tim Seamans](https://www.linkedin.com/in/timseamans?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Ftimseamans_ai-fluency-has-become-a-big-priority-for-activity-7406713442818228224-qAmc&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  AI fluency has become a big priority for me. And honestly, it should be for anyone hoping to weave AI into their daily workflow. But getting there wasn’t as simple as handing people a tool and saying “go.” So our team built a three-tiered learning system. Level One is for everyone, because true AI fluency starts with something simple: comfort. Comfort with the concepts, the language, the possibilities. What surprised me most? The overwhelming interest in certification. People aren’t just curious about how AI works; they want to understand how it benefits them, their role, and their growth. And that’s where this model has really shined. When people learn AI in a way that feels personal and relevant, they put it into context faster, apply it faster, and see real value faster. The internet may offer a million directions for AI. But make it practical, make it human, make it theirs— And people are all in.

* [Chris Reynolds](https://www.linkedin.com/in/chrismreynolds?trk=public_post_feed-actor-name)

  + [Report this post](/uas/login?session_redirect=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.linkedin.com%2Fposts%2Fchrismreynolds_i-cant-tell-where-learning-ends-and-building-activity-7412867059962982400-X73B&trk=public_post_ellipsis-menu-semaphore-sign-in-redirect&guestReportContentType=POST&_f=guest-reporting)

  I can't tell where learning ends and building begins when I'm working with AI anymore—and I think that's exactly why I find it so addicting. Think about how learning has always worked:   → You learn something → Then you pause → You create something → Then you pause again   There's always been this gap between input and output. But AI shrinks it to nothing.   Now, you can go right from learning into creating and back and forth again as many times as you want. The learning can be as superficial or intense as you want.   I use these AI tools to take on super challenging problems every single day. And what's so amazing is that as I'm learning how something works, I'm also building the solution.   It's like traditional learning on freaking steroids.   And if you know how to use it, you can move faster and do more than you ever thought possible.

# Zara's Newsletter

# AI and the "courage to learn"

### Confusion no longer means defeat

Zara Zhang's avatar

# [Zara's Newsletter](/)

# AI and the "courage to learn"

### Confusion no longer means defeat

![Zara Zhang's avatar](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5kDb!,w_36,h_36,c_fill,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F43e911ad-1861-427f-8494-18f0e3376e2c_972x972.png)

Learning feels like climbing a glass mountain—smooth progress until you hit a slick spot with no handhold, and suddenly you're sliding back down. For most of my life, I divided the world into mountains I could climb (writing, languages) and those I couldn't (programming, math). Then AI handed me something remarkable: invisible handholds that appear precisely when I need them. The mountains haven't changed—but suddenly, they're all climbable.

For years, I boxed myself in as a "humanities person." Anything STEM-related sat firmly outside my comfort zone. Each time I attempted a CS problem set or math homework, a familiar voice of self-doubt would emerge: "Can I actually do this?" Without a guide to navigate the confusion, I often retreated to safer intellectual territory. The mental barrier wasn't about capability but belief and support.

Contrast this with my experience learning languages or writing. There, I moved with confidence, even enthusiasm. The difference wasn't the subject matter itself, but my relationship with it.

**We only truly enjoy learning when we believe something is** ***learnable*****.** This is where AI's transformative power lies—not just in answering questions, but in making knowledge accessible in ways that convince us **anything can be learned**.

Consider what happens now when learning anxiety strikes. Confused about a concept? A 24/7 teacher stands ready to explain it in whatever way makes sense to you. Don't know where to begin with a new subject? AI can map out a personalized study plan. Watched a tutorial but missed a crucial point? AI can explain it like you're five. Want to build something but feel overwhelmed by technical choices? AI can simplify your options.

Since integrating AI into my learning process, I've ventured further outside my intellectual comfort zone than ever before (since college). I'm watching coding lectures. I'm building products hands-on. I'm exploring concepts I didn't know existed just months ago. Most surprisingly, **I've rediscovered the fundamental pleasure of learning.**

There's a distinct feeling that washes over you when you sit with a new concept and think, "With this AI companion, I can actually figure this out." It's a blend of curiosity, excitement, and possibility—the joy of learning amplified by the knowledge that confusion is just a prompt away.

The most profound shift isn't that AI makes information available—the internet already did that. It's that AI makes knowledge accessible, which nurtures the belief that everything is learnable. And when we believe we can learn something, we usually do.

# Zara stocks up on AI, big data, analytics partnerships and investments

Published June 18, 2018

By

[Alex Hickey](/users/ahickey/)
Associate Editor

* [![Share this article](/static/img/social_icons/share-icon.svg?313321260226)

### Dive Brief:

* Zara is incorporating artificial intelligence, automation and big data into its business strategy and supply chain in a bid to stay ahead of competitors, [reports Reuters](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-inditex-technology-focus/zara-looks-to-technology-to-keep-up-with-faster-fashion-idUSKBN1JB0HG). The fashion retailer is hiring talent from startups and partnering with Jetlore, which offers an AI-powered consumer behavior prediction platform, and El Arte de Medir, a Spanish big data company.
* The innovation unit of Zara’s parent company, Inditex, is working with Intel on devices to measure clothing volume in boxes and Fetch Robotics on robot deployment for stock inventory, according to Reuters. Micro-chips from Tyco, an alarm provider, are also used to track product data and locations along the supply chain.
* Last week, Inditex [posted record first quarter sales](https://www.retaildive.com/news/zara-fights-off-currency-blows-with-record-q1-sales/525704/) of more than $6.7 billion, with positive same-store sales growth across all geographies.

### Dive Insight:

Retailers are betting on customer-facing technologies, such as augmented reality applications, virtual assistants and AI-powered chatbots, to keep up with the herd of increasingly digital-savvy retailers. But the technology going on behind the scenes and on the business side is just as important as what the customer experiences.

The retail Internet of Things market is projected to [reach $94 billion by 2025](https://www.retaildive.com/news/retail-iot-could-be-94-billion-market-by-2025/525578/), improving accountability and management in supply chains and creating more touch points to measure customer data and behavior. Meanwhile retail spending on AI is expected to [reach $7.3 billion by 2022](https://www.retaildive.com/news/retail-spending-on-ai-to-reach-73b-by-2022/516170/) as more businesses onboard automation, chatbot, personalization and analytics solutions, among others.

Competitors like H&M are looking at [advanced analytics, new technologies and supply chain optimization](https://www.retaildive.com/news/HM-turnaround-runs-through-supply-chain/520704/) to turn financials around and build out resilience. Zara has continued to out-do its rivals, adjusting to brick-and-mortar needs and supporting integrated store-online models, and keeping abreast of advanced technologies will facilitate the “full integration between store and online stock rooms” that Inditex CEO Pablo Isla is pursuing.

Raw Content: # [Zara's Newsletter](/)

# I learned more about AI from YouTube than Harvard

### I'm attending YouTube University for a degree in Practical AI

[Zara Zhang](https://substack.com/@zarazhang)

Mar 03, 2025

I got my bachelor's degree from Harvard University, but I got all my practical AI knowledge from YouTube University.

Last weekend, I spent 3.5 hours watching Andrej Karpathy's [latest lecture](https://www.youtube.com/watch?si=vklmn0V2N_B5IegC&v=7xTGNNLPyMI&feature=youtu.be) on how LLMs work. It wasn't just the most productive time I've spent online in the past year—it was more valuable than anything I learned in Harvard classrooms.

The abundance of free, high-quality knowledge about AI on YouTube is astounding. With a few clicks, I can turn the world's leading AI practitioners into my personal, on-demand tutors.

My "YouTube University" curriculum reads like a who's who of tech visionaries:

* Learned how LLMs work from world-class AI researchers like [Andrej Karpathy](https://www.youtube.com/@AndrejKarpathy)
* Explored AI's impact on the future of work through conversations with [Satya Nadella](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4GLSzuYXh6w)
* Discovered AI's societal implications through [Fei-Fei Li's insights](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0jMgskLxw3s)
* Studied how to build startups in the AI era from founders of [Perplexity](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=r1Bi10Xt0fc) and [Harvey](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0DDS8jWqEPg)
* Learned about how AI is revolutionizing coding from the founders of [Cursor](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=oFfVt3S51T4)
* Absorbed AI-native product design principles from the minds behind [NotebookLM](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sOyFpSW1Vls) and [Granola](https://youtu.be/qq3x23MfxkI?si=Hqnal41CtjIqnqNa)
* Glimpsed the future of AI agents through researchers at [OpenAI](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bNEvJYzoa8A) and [Anthropic](https://youtu.be/LP5OCa20Zpg?si=Vg8pYeyDPt7fyoa1)
* …

I used to be perplexed by the popularity of long-form video podcasts—those hour-long interviews with little more visual interest than two talking heads. Who has the patience for that?

Turns out, I do. Nearly all my practical AI knowledge—and countless "aha" moments—have come from spending undistracted time with these in-depth conversations.

I often enter a state of "flow" while watching these knowledge-dense videos, similar to what I experience when reading a good book. The field of AI is evolving so rapidly that most cutting-edge knowledge hasn't been captured in books yet. These video podcasts fill that gap, becoming the equivalent of essential reading.

I'm immensely grateful to the creators and educators who share such high-quality content freely online. This culture of open knowledge sharing represents what I love most about Silicon Valley.

When a technological revolution arrives, it resets the playing field: **Because everyone needs to learn something new, fancy degrees matter less than curiosity and drive.** What you need isn't an elite university acceptance letter—it's an internet connection and the willingness to learn.

The great equalizer of our time isn't just AI itself, but the democratized access to learning about it.

**P.S. Here’s a list of the specific YouTube channels that I’ve found to be the most useful in my AI learning journey:**

Shows focused on AI:

* [AI and I](https://www.youtube.com/@EveryInc)
* [Latent Space](https://www.youtube.com/@LatentSpacePod)
* [Unsupervised learning (RedPoint Capital)](https://www.youtube.com/@RedpointAI)
* [Training data (Sequoia Capital)](https://youtube.com/playlist?list=PLOhHNjZItNnMm5tdW61JpnyxeYH5NDDx8&si=oW2IZDQ74TPxLKw8)
* [Google DeepMind the Podcast](https://youtube.com/playlist?list=PLqYmG7hTraZBiUr6_Qf8YTS2Oqy3OGZEj&si=0g_-48g2FF14ksux)
* [No priors](https://www.youtube.com/@NoPriorsPodcast)
* [AI+a16z](https://youtube.com/playlist?list=PLM4u6XbiXf5p-bokWYcyi8SKnMzoYW4b3&si=7YGQurcbAnvYWlco)
* [The AI Daily Brief](https://www.youtube.com/@AIDailyBrief)

Shows focused on broader startup/product building, but often feature AI luminaries as guests:

* [Lex Fridman](https://www.youtube.com/@lexfridman)
* [Dwarkesh Podcast](https://www.youtube.com/@DwarkeshPatel)
* [Lenny's Podcast](https://www.youtube.com/@LennysPodcast)
* [Peter Yang](https://www.youtube.com/@PeterYangYT)
* [The Logan Bartlett show](https://www.youtube.com/@theloganbartlettshow)
* [Minus One (South Park Commons)](https://youtube.com/playlist?list=PLmYVYFmFwGm3txxUduawn7i53C5rDjjd7&si=mWdX1yx0hImiaxC5)
* [Lightcone podcast (Y Combinator)](https://youtube.com/playlist?list=PLQ-uHSnFig5Ob4XXhgSK26Smb4oRhzFmK&si=6c_4yOOMzyy3CUKv)
* [Hallway Chat](https://www.youtube.com/@HallwayChat)

Zara is deploying AI to generate fashion images by modifying real models' photos to display different outfits, accelerating content creation. Models approve edits and receive pay equivalent to conducting additional shoots. H&M and Zalando are testing similar workflows, positioning AI as a complement to—not replacement for—creative teams.
